reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "gpt2"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 32
batch_size = 1024

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

A typical transformer block is: ``` x = x + attn(ln1(x)) x = x + mlp(ln2(x)) ``` Where: ``` attn(x) = proj(attention(qkv(x))) mlp(x) = proj2(gelu(proj1(x))) ``` If we use PyTorch ops, each of these is multiple kernel launches. If we fuse, we can reduce kernel launch overhead and improve memory bandwidth. For H100 with 3.35 TB/s memory bandwidth and FP32 compute of 67 TFLOPS, the arithmetic intensity needed to be compute-bound is: 67 TFLOPS / 3.35 TB/s = 20 FLOPs/byte For a [32768, 768] @ [768, 3072] GEMM: - Input bytes: 32768*768*4 + 768*3072*4 ≈ 100MB + 9MB = 109MB - FLOPs: 32768*768*3072*2 ≈ 155 GFLOPs - Arithmetic intensity: 155G / 109M ≈ 1422 FLOPs/byte → definitely compute bound for cuBLAS So the big GEMMs are compute-bound and cuBLAS is near-optimal. Minimize memory allocations by reusing buffers For the attention kernel with S=32, causal mask, multi-head: - Grid: (batch_size, num_heads) - Each block handles one batch item and one head - Load Q, K, V into shared memory - Q is [S, head_dim], K is [S, head_dim], V is [S, head_dim] - Compute attention scores [S, S] in registers (32x32 = 1024 floats per thread... too many) - Actually, each thread can compute one row of the attention matrix. With S=32 and block size 32, each thread computes 32 dot products of length 64, then softmax, then 32 dot products of length 32 with V. Wait, with head_dim=64 and S=32: - 32 threads per block (one per row) - Each thread loads 64 floats of Q, 64 floats of K (shared), 64 floats of V (shared) - For softmax, each thread computes 32 dot products, then does softmax over 32 elements - Then each thread computes 64 outputs - Shared memory: K needs 32*64 floats = 8KB, V needs 32*64 = 8KB, Q can be in registers or shared. Total ~16KB per block. Very feasible. With B=1024 and num_heads=12, total blocks = 12288. H100 has 132 SMs, so ~93 blocks per SM. This is plenty to keep the GPU busy. For the causal mask: since S=32 is small, each thread just skips the appropriate columns. Let me design the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* qkv, // [B, S, 3*H] or already split const float* attn_mask, // causal mask [S, S] or nullptr float* out, // [B, S, H] int B, int S, int H, int num_heads ) { int batch = blockIdx.x; int head = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int head_dim = H / num_heads; if (tid >= S) return; // Pointers to Q, K, V for this batch/head const float* q = qkv + batch * S * 3 * H + head * head_dim; const float* k = q + H; // K is after Q const float* v = k + H; // V is after K // Shared memory for K and V (to load once and reuse) __shared__ float shared_k[32 * 64]; // S * head_dim __shared__ float shared_v[32 * 64]; // Load K and V into shared memory // Each thread loads head_dim/S portions... or each thread loads its column // Actually, let each thread load one row for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { shared_k[tid * head_dim + d] = k[tid * 3 * H + d]; shared_v[tid * head_dim + d] = v[tid * 3 * H + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Q for this row float q_vec[64]; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_vec[d] = q[tid * 3 * H + d]; } // Compute attention scores float scores[32]; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(head_dim); for (int j = 0; j <= tid; j++) { // causal: j <= tid float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { sum += q_vec[d] * shared_k[j * head_dim + d]; } scores[j] = sum * scale; } for (int j = tid + 1; j < S; j++) { scores[j] = -INFINITY; } // Softmax float max_score = scores[0]; for (int j = 1; j < S; j++) { if (scores[j] > max_score) max_score = scores[j]; } float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < S; j++) { scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score); if (j <= tid) exp_sum += scores[j]; // only sum valid ones } float inv_sum = 1.0f / exp_sum; for (int j = 0; j <= tid; j++) { scores[j] *= inv_sum; } // Compute output float out_vec[64]; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_vec[d] = 0.0f; } for (int j = 0; j <= tid; j++) { for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_vec[d] += scores[j] * shared_v[j * head_dim + d]; } } // Write output float* out_ptr = out + batch * S * H + head * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_ptr[tid * H + d] = out_vec[d]; } } ``` Wait, there's an issue with memory layout. If QKV is [B, S, 3*H], then: - Q is at offset [b, s, 0:H] - K is at offset [b, s, H:2H] - V is at offset [b, s, 2H:3H] So for head h, Q starts at [b, s, h*head_dim]. Actually, GPT-2 doesn't use head splitting in the attention projection weights. The c_attn weight is [H, 3H], and after projection, we split into Q, K, V along the last dimension. The output is [B, S, 3H]. We then split into Q, K, V each of [B, S, H]. Then Q, K, V are reshaped to [B, num_heads, S, head_dim] via transpose. In the HuggingFace implementation, the split is done with `.split(self.split_size, dim=2)`, which creates views (no copy). Then `reshape` is used. But these reshape operations have kernel launch overhead. My custom kernel can take the [B, S, 3H] tensor directly and avoid the split/reshape overhead. Actually, I need to be more careful. GPT-2 uses Conv1d for the linear layers, not nn.Linear. The weights are transposed. But at our sequence length, Conv1d with kernel_size=1 is equivalent to a linear layer. In HF's GPT-2, `c_attn` is a `Conv1D` with weight shape [H, 3H] (note: this is actually the transposed shape, so the matmul is `x @ self.weight + self.bias`). So for `x` of shape [B*S, H], the output is `x @ weight + bias`, where weight is [H, 3H]. OK let me just write the code and compile it to see if there are errors. Let me draft the code structure: ```python class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() # Load model to extract weights model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) model.eval() # Extract weights and buffers as parameters/buffers self.wte = model.transformer.wte.weight.detach() self.wpe = model.transformer.wpe.weight.detach() self.num_layers = config.n_layer self.num_heads = config.n_head self.hidden_size = config.n_embd self.head_dim = self.hidden_size // self.num_heads self.vocab_size = config.vocab_size # ... extract all layer weights ... # Preallocate work buffers # buffer1, buffer2, buffer3 for [B, S, H] and [B, S, 4H] shapes # Load custom CUDA kernels # ... def forward(self, x): B, S = x.shape # embeddings # loop over layers # lm head return logits ``` For custom kernels, I'll write: 1. `fused_embedding(input_ids, wte, wpe, output)` 2. `fused_layer_norm(input, weight, bias, output)` 3. `fused_attention(qkv, output, num_heads, head_dim)` - takes [B, S, 3H] output from c_attn, computes attention, writes [B, S, H] 4. `fused_bias_gelu(input, bias, output)` 5. `final_output_projection` etc. Wait, for attention, I need to handle the causal mask. For GPT-2, the causal mask is typically created once and cached. I can create it in `__init__` or compute it in the kernel. For S=32, the causal mask is 32x32. In the kernel, I can just check `if (col <= row)` since the mask is just causal. Actually, in GPT-2, the attention scores are also sometimes modified by `attn_mask` which is an additive mask (0 for attend, -inf for don't attend). Now for the Kernel code, I need to be careful about memory layout. In PyTorch, a tensor of shape [B, S, H] is stored row-major (B is outermost, H is innermost, contiguous). So element [b, s, h] is at index `b * S * H + s * H + h`. But wait, after matmul with Conv1D weight [H, 3H], the output qkv is [B, S, 3H]. For my custom attention kernel, Q, K, V are interleaved in the last dimension: - Q for position [b, s] is at `qkv[b, s, 0:H]` - K is at `qkv[b, s, H:2H]` - V is at `qkv[b, s, 2H:3H]` In the attention kernel I'll work with [B, S, 3H] directly. Let me draft the attention kernel more carefully: ```cuda __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* qkv, float* out, int B, int S, int H, int num_heads ) { int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; int head_idx = blockIdx.y; int seq_idx = threadIdx.x; if (seq_idx >= S) return; int head_dim = H / num_heads; // Offset to this batch item const float* batch_qkv = qkv + batch_idx * S * 3 * H; float* batch_out = out + batch_idx * S * H; // Load Q for this sequence position and head float q[64]; // head_dim max const float* q_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + head_idx * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q[d] = q_ptr[d]; } // Shared memory for K and V of this head for all sequence positions extern __shared__ float shared_kv[]; float* shared_k = shared_kv; float* shared_v = shared_kv + S * head_dim; // Load K and V: each thread loads its sequence position const float* k_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + H + head_idx * head_dim; const float* v_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + 2 * H + head_idx * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { shared_k[seq_idx * head_dim + d] = k_ptr[d]; shared_v[seq_idx * head_dim + d] = v_ptr[d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention scores float scores[32]; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q[d] * shared_k[j * head_dim + d]; } scores[j] = dot * scale; } for (int j = seq_idx + 1; j < S; j++) { scores[j] = -1e9f; // -inf equivalent } // Softmax float max_val = scores[0]; for (int j = 1; j < S; j++) { if (scores[j] > max_val) max_val = scores[j]; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_val); sum_exp += scores[j]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { scores[j] *= inv_sum; } // Compute output float out_val[64] = {0}; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_val[d] += scores[j] * shared_v[j * head_dim + d]; } } // Write output float* out_ptr = batch_out + seq_idx * H + head_idx * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_ptr[d] = out_val[d]; } } ``` I need to dynamically size shared memory based on head_dim. Actually, for GPT-2, head_dim = 64, S = 32. So shared memory = 2 * 32 * 64 * 4 = 16,384 bytes. That's small. But wait, in CUDA C, I can't use variable-length arrays like `float q[head_dim]`. I need to either: 1. Use a fixed max size (e.g., 64 or 128) 2. Or unroll with preprocessor Actually, for S=32, head_dim=64, I can just use fixed sizes or pass them as template parameters. With 32 threads per block, that's 8KB total. That's fine. Actually, for the attention scores, I can compute them on the fly without storing in an array: ```cuda // Compute max score first float max_score = -1e9f; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q[d] * shared_k[j * head_dim + d]; } float score = dot * scale; if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } // Compute sum of exps float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q[d] * shared_k[j * head_dim + d]; } float score = expf(dot * scale - max_score); sum_exp += score; } // Compute weighted sum float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; float out_val[128] = {0}; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q[d] * shared_k[j * head_dim + d]; } float weight = expf(dot * scale - max_score) * inv_sum; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_val[d] += weight * shared_v[j * head_dim + d]; } } ``` This does redundant computation (3 passes over Q·K) but avoids arrays and might be cache-friendly since S=32. With head_dim=64, each dot product is 64 mul-adds = 128 FLOPs. For each sequence position, we do this ~3 * 32 * 128 = 12,288 FLOPs. For 1024 batches and 12 heads = 12,288 threads, that's 151M FLOPs for attention, which is tiny. The bottleneck is memory, so recomputing is fine if it saves register space. Actually, let me reconsider the attention scores array. For local arrays, I'll use `#define MAX_HEAD_DIM 128` and `#define MAX_SEQ_LEN 128`. Actually, one more optimization: instead of each thread loading its own row of K and V, I should think about coalescing. Each thread reads `head_dim` consecutive floats. With 32 threads, thread i reads `shared_k[i*head_dim + d]`. The memory access pattern is: - Thread 0: elements [0, 1, 2, ..., head_dim-1] - Thread 1: elements [head_dim, head_dim+1, ...] This is coalesced if `head_dim` is aligned! Since head_dim=64, each thread reads 64 contiguous floats = 256 bytes. With float4 or similar, this is coalesced. For writing to shared memory: `shared_k[seq_idx * head_dim + d]` - thread seq_idx writes to `seq_idx * head_dim + d`. This is also coalesged per d. OK, let me start writing the full code. I'll need: 1. Embedding kernel 2. LayerNorm kernel 3. Attention kernel 4. Bias + GELU kernel 5. Residual add kernel 6. A final linear layer (could use matmul) 7. LM head matmul For the LayerNorm kernel: ```cuda __global__ void layer_norm_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, float* residual, // if not null, add residual before norm int N, int hidden_size ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; const float* x = input + idx * hidden_size; float* y = output + idx * hidden_size; float mean = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < hidden_size; i++) { float val = x[i]; if (residual != nullptr) val += residual[idx * hidden_size + i]; mean += val; } mean /= hidden_size; float var = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < hidden_size; i++) { float val = x[i]; if (residual != nullptr) val += residual[idx * hidden_size + i]; var += (val - mean) * (val - mean); } var /= hidden_size; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); for (int i = 0; i < hidden_size; i++) { float val = x[i]; if (residual != nullptr) val += residual[idx * hidden_size + i]; y[i] = (val - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i]; } } ``` Wait, this adds residual before layer norm. Now let me think about the exact HF GPT-2 implementation to match numerically: 1. **Embeddings**: ```python inputs_embeds = self.wte(input_ids) position_embeds = self.wpe(position_ids) hidden_states = inputs_embeds + position_embeds ``` 2. **Attention**: ```python # ln_1 hidden_states = self.ln_1(hidden_states) # c_attn query, key, value = self.c_attn(hidden_states).split(self.split_size, dim=2) query = self._split_heads(query, self.num_heads, self.head_dim) key = self._split_heads(key, self.num_heads, self.head_dim) value = self._split_heads(value, self.num_heads, self.head_dim) # attn attn_output, attn_weights = self._attn(query, key, value, attention_mask, head_mask) attn_output = self._merge_heads(attn_output) attn_output = self.c_proj(attn_output) attn_output = self.attn_dropout(attn_output) # residual hidden_states = hidden_states + attn_output # Wait, no. In GPT2Block: # The standard is: hidden_states = hidden_states + attn_output # But hidden_states was overwritten by ln_1! ``` Actually, looking at `modeling_gpt2.py`: ```python def forward(self, hidden_states, ...): residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.ln_1(hidden_states) attn_outputs = self.attn(hidden_states, ...) attn_output = attn_outputs[0] # residual connection hidden_states = attn_output + residual # ... residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.ln_2(hidden_states) feed_forward_hidden_states = self.mlp(hidden_states) hidden_states = residual + feed_forward_hidden_states ``` So the layer norm is applied first, then the sublayer, then residual add. For attention: ```python def _attn(self, query, key, value, attention_mask=None, head_mask=None): attn_weights = torch.matmul(query, key.transpose(-1, -2)) if self.scale_attn_weights: attn_weights = attn_weights / torch.full( [], value.size(-1) ** 0.5, dtype=attn_weights.dtype, device=attn_weights.device ) # causal mask query_length, key_length = query.size(-2), key.size(-2) causal_mask = self.bias[:, :, key_length - query_length : key_length, :key_length] mask_value = torch.finfo(attn_weights.dtype).min mask_value = torch.full([], mask_value, dtype=attn_weights.dtype, device=attn_weights.device) attn_weights = torch.where(causal_mask, attn_weights.to(attn_weights.dtype), mask_value) attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1) attn_weights = attn_weights.type(value.dtype) attn_weights = self.attn_dropout(attn_weights) attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_weights, value) return attn_output, attn_weights ``` Key things: 1. Scale by sqrt(head_dim) 2. Causal mask (triangular, True=keep, False=mask) 3. Softmax over last dim 4. matmul with value 5. Note: attn_weights are first computed, then causal mask applied using `torch.where` The causal mask `self.bias` is registered as a buffer of shape [1, 1, 1024, 1024] where it's True in the lower triangle. `mask_value` is the dtype minimum (like -inf for float). In my kernel, I'll just set upper triangle to a large negative number before softmax. The exact value matters for numerical precision. HF uses `torch.finfo(dtype).min`, which for float32 is approximately -3.4028235e+38. I should use a very negative number like -1e9 or exactly match. Wait, GPT-2 has `scale_attn_weights = True` by default. And it applies a causal mask. For numerical matching, I need to be careful about: 1. Order of operations (though matmul is deterministic in cuBLAS... actually not always, but for the same inputs, same order) 2. Softmax implementation 3. LayerNorm implementation 4. GELU implementation For GELU, HF GPT-2 uses the `NewGELUActivation`: ```python def forward(self, input: Tensor) -> Tensor: return 0.5 * input * (1.0 + torch.tanh(math.sqrt(2.0 / math.pi) * (input + 0.044715 * torch.pow(input, 3.0)))) ``` Not the `erf` version! This is the tanh approximation. I need to match exactly. For LayerNorm, HF uses `nn.LayerNorm` which computes: ```python mean = x.mean(-1, keepdim=True) var = x.var(-1, keepdim=True, unbiased=False) # NOTE: unbiased=False! y = (x - mean) / torch.sqrt(var + self.eps) * self.weight + self.bias ``` IMPORTANT: `unbiased=False`! The variance is computed with division by N, not N-1. And eps=1e-5. For attention: scale factor is `1/sqrt(head_dim)`. The causal mask sets masked elements to a very negative value, then softmax. After softmax, attention weights are in float32. Wait, in my custom kernel, I'll compute the exact same operations. OK, now for the attention kernel, I need to be extra careful about numerical precision if we want to match within 1e-4. Custom softmax in CUDA needs to match PyTorch's softmax. PyTorch's softmax computes: ```python max_val = x.max(dim=-1, keepdim=True) exp(x - max_val) sum_exp = exp_x.sum(dim=-1, keepdim=True) result = exp_x / sum_exp ``` In my kernel, I'll do the same. For the causal mask, I need to replace masked positions with a very negative number. In PyTorch, HF uses: ```python mask_value = torch.finfo(attn_weights.dtype).min # -3.4028234663852886e+38 for float32 attn_weights = torch.where(causal_mask, attn_weights, mask_value) ``` If I just set masked values to -1e9, the softmax exp(-1e9) will underflow to 0.0 in float32, which is correct. The issue is whether exp(-1e9) is exactly 0.0. In float32, exp(-89) underflows to 0, so exp(-1e9) = 0.0. So -1e9 is safe. But to match numerically, I should probably use exactly the same mask value. Actually, if masked positions softmax to exactly 0, and PyTorch also gets 0, then the unmasked positions should be identical (up to small differences from the max/sum computation). Let me use `-10000.0f` or a similar large negative number to be safe. For exact matching, I might want to compute the scale factor outside and pass it in. `1/sqrt(64) = 0.125`. Actually `sqrt(64) = 8`, so `1/8 = 0.125`. That's exact in float32. Great. For the `attn_weights.type(value.dtype)` step in HF, it's converting from float32 to... well, it's already float32 in our case. And the `attn_dropout` is applied. Let me draft the kernels and code. For the `fused_bias_gelu` kernel, matching HF's NewGELU: ```cuda __global__ void fused_bias_gelu_kernel( const float* input, const float* bias, float* output, int N, int feature_size ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * feature_size; if (idx >= total) return; int f = idx % feature_size; float x = input[idx] + bias[f]; // NewGELU: 0.5 * x * (1.0 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3))) float c = 0.044715f; float sqrt_2_over_pi = 0.7978845608028654f; float x3 = x * x * x; float t = sqrt_2_over_pi * (x + c * x3); float tanh_t = tanhf(t); float y = 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanh_t); output[idx] = y; } ``` For `fused_residual_add`: ```cuda __global__ void residual_add_kernel( const float* residual, const float* other, float* output, int N ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; output[idx] = residual[idx] + other[idx]; } ``` For `fused_embedding`: ```cuda __global__ void embedding_kernel( const int64_t* input_ids, const float* wte, const float* wpe, float* output, int B, int S, int H ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * S * H; if (idx >= total) return; int h = idx % H; int s = (idx / H) % S; int b = idx / (S * H); int64_t token_id = input_ids[b * S + s]; output[idx] = wte[token_id * H + h] + wpe[s * H + h]; } ``` For `fused_layer_norm`: ```cuda __global__ void layer_norm_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int N, int H ) { int n = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; const float* x = input + n * H; float* y = output + n * H; float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) { sum += x[i]; } float mean = sum / H; float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) { float diff = x[i] - mean; sq_sum += diff * diff; } float var = sq_sum / H; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) { y[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i]; } } ``` For attention, as discussed earlier. But wait, there's a subtlety. In HF's attention, Q, K, V are split from the [B, S, 3H] tensor, then reshaped to [B, num_heads, S, head_dim]. In CUDA, the tensor is row-major [B, S, 3H], so Q is at offset 0, K at H, V at 2H within the last dimension. For my attention kernel, I'll read Q, K, V directly from the [B, S, 3H] tensor without creating intermediate tensors. For the output, I'll write to [B, S, H] which will then be used in the next matmul. One thing: my attention kernel needs to support arbitrary batch size. Let me make sure. Also, I need to think about whether to pass `num_heads` and `head_dim` at runtime or hardcode. I'll pass them. Let me write the attention kernel with dynamic shared memory: ```cuda #define MAX_SEQ_LEN 128 #define MAX_HEAD_DIM 128 __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* qkv, float* out, int B, int S, int H, int num_heads ) { int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; int head_idx = blockIdx.y; int seq_idx = threadIdx.x; if (seq_idx >= S || batch_idx >= B || head_idx >= num_heads) return; int head_dim = H / num_heads; const float* batch_qkv = qkv + batch_idx * S * 3 * H; float* batch_out = out + batch_idx * S * H; // Load Q for this thread's sequence position and head float q_val[MAX_HEAD_DIM]; const float* q_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + head_idx * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q_val[d] = q_ptr[d]; } // Shared memory for K and V extern __shared__ float shared_kv[]; float* shared_k = shared_kv; float* shared_v = shared_kv + S * head_dim; // Load K and V for this sequence position and head const float* k_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + H + head_idx * head_dim; const float* v_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + 2 * H + head_idx * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { shared_k[seq_idx * head_dim + d] = k_ptr[d]; shared_v[seq_idx * head_dim + d] = v_ptr[d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention scores float scores[MAX_SEQ_LEN]; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q_val[d] * shared_k[j * head_dim + d]; } scores[j] = dot * scale; } for (int j = seq_idx + 1; j < S; j++) { scores[j] = -1e9f; // causal mask } // Softmax float max_score = scores[0]; for (int j = 1; j < S; j++) { if (scores[j] > max_score) max_score = scores[j]; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[j]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { scores[j] *= inv_sum; } // Compute output float out_val[MAX_HEAD_DIM]; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_val[d] = 0.0f; } for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_val[d] += scores[j] * shared_v[j * head_dim + d]; } } // Write output float* out_ptr = batch_out + seq_idx * H + head_idx * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_ptr[d] = out_val[d]; } } ``` Wait, I need to be careful. In GPT-2, the MLP uses `Conv1D` which stores weights in a transposed manner. The operation is `x @ weight + bias`. When using `torch.matmul`, I need to make sure the weight orientation is correct. Actually, HF's `Conv1D` stores weight as [in_features, out_features]. So for `c_attn`, weight is [768, 2304]. When calling `torch.matmul(x, weight)` where `x` is [..., 768], the output is [..., 2304]. This is the same as a linear layer with transposed weight. In my code, I'll use `torch.matmul`: ```python qkv = torch.matmul(hidden_states, attn_weight) + attn_bias ``` Where `hidden_states` is [B, S, H] and `attn_weight` is [H, 3H]. My kernel implements: compute Q·K per row -> scale -> causal mask -> softmax -> matmul with V. This is exactly the same computation, assuming dropout=0. For numerical matching, the main challenges are: 1. Order of summation in the dot products ( CUDA might sum in different order than cuBLAS, causing tiny differences) 2. Softmax implementation 3. This means writing a custom elementwise kernel for bias+GELU might not help at all, or could hurt. What CAN I fuse then? 1. **LayerNorm + something**? LayerNorm is memory-bound. In HF, it's `layer_norm(x)` then `matmul`. I can't fuse these without a custom matmul. 2. **Residual add + LayerNorm**? In HF: `residual = x`, then `x = attn_output + x` (residual), then `x = layer_norm(x)`. That's two separate memory passes. I could fuse: `out = layer_norm(attn_output + residual)` into one pass. This saves ~100MB of memory traffic per layer. With 12 layers, that's 1.2GB saved, which is ~0.36ms at 3.35TB/s. Not huge but not zero. 3. **Attention softmax + masking + scale**: In HF, these are often separate or partially fused. `torch.where` for masking and `F.softmax` for softmax are separate kernels. Writing a kernel that does `apply_causal_mask + softmax` in one pass saves one read+write of the attention scores. For attention scores [B, num_heads, S, S] = [1024, 12, 32, 32] = 12.5M floats = 50MB. - Reference: scores Q@K^T (matmul, compute) -> scale (elementwise) -> causal mask (where) -> softmax -> dropout After matmul: write 50MB Scale: read 50MB, write 50MB Mask: read 50MB, write 50MB Softmax: read 50MB, write 50MB Dropout (eval: 0): no-op Total after matmul: 300MB memory traffic - Custom kernel: takes Q, K, computes scores, applies mask, softmax, all in one kernel without writing intermediate scores to global memory. Plus the reference writes the scores tensor (50MB) which my custom kernel avoids. But wait, the reference's matmul for Q@K^T already writes 50MB. My custom kernel computes Q@K^T internally (in registers), never writes to global memory, then does softmax, then writes the attention output. Wait, but in my custom kernel I take QKV [B, S, 3H] and I need to load Q and K into shared memory and compute dot products. The Q and K are already computed by the matmul. Plus fewer kernel launches. And for compute, the dot products in my custom kernel are negligible compared to the matmuls, so compute time should be similar. This means my custom attention kernel could provide a very meaningful speedup! Now, the numerical matching concern. The custom kernel computes Q·K manually with 64-element dot products. The reference uses cuBLAS for this. For float32, the difference between a sequential dot product and cuBLAS's tiled summation is typically very small. With 64 elements, the maximum error is bounded by about `n * eps * max_val` where n=64, eps=1e-7, giving ~6e-6 relative error. In the context of attention followed by softmax, this gets normalized. After 12 layers, it might accumulate. But since each layer's attention output gets mixed with other layers and the residual connection, the error might not compound too badly. Let me think about a worst case. If the dot product error is 1e-5 per element, and this affects the attention weights by 1e-5, and the output is a weighted sum of values, the error per output element might be ~1e-5 * head_dim ≈ 1e-3? No, that overestimates. The softmax ensures weights sum to 1, so errors in weights are bounded. Actually, let me think more carefully. Suppose the true score for a token is s, and my kernel computes s + δ. After softmax, the weight is exp(s+δ) / Σexp(s_j+δ_j). The derivative of softmax with respect to input is w_i(1-w_i), so a small error δ in score i causes a change in weight i of approximately w_i(1-w_i) * δ. For typical attention, weights might be 0.1 to 0.5. So weight error is at most ~0.25 * δ. If δ ≈ 1e-5 (from dot product), then weight error is ~2.5e-6. Multiplying by V values (maybe ~1), and summing 32 of them: error ~8e-5. Across 12 layers with residual connections: final error might be ~1e-3? That might exceed 1e-4. Hmm, 1e-3 is concerning. But this is a very rough estimate. The actual error might be smaller. And some errors might cancel out. I could reduce the error by using a different summation strategy in the custom kernel. For example, Kahan summation for the dot product would essentially eliminate the error at the cost of a few extra operations per element. Let me modify the kernel to use Kahan summation: ```cuda float dot = 0.0f; float c = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float y = q_val[d] * shared_k[j * head_dim + d] - c; float t = dot + y; c = (t - dot) - y; dot = t; } ``` This adds a few more operations per iteration but significantly improves accuracy. For head_dim=64, the overhead is small. Alternatively, I can compute the dot product in chunks and sum the chunks with higher precision. But Kahan is simple and effective. Another approach: sort the products before summing. Not practical. Or: use `float2` or `float4` and do pairwise summation. Still complex. I'll use Kahan summation for the dot products in my attention kernel. This should make the results very close to cuBLAS. For the softmax, I'll use the same algorithm as PyTorch (subtract max, exp, divide by sum). The only difference might be the exact `max` computation. In PyTorch, max is computed over the tensor. In my kernel, each thread computes max over its row. Since there are only 32 elements per row, and each thread computes its own max, it should be identical to PyTorch's row-wise max (which also does row-wise max). For the causal mask, I need to match exactly. HF sets masked positions to `torch.finfo(dtype).min`. For float32, this is `-3.4028234663852886e+38`. I'll use the same exact value: ```cuda #define NEG_INF -3.4028234663852886e+38f ``` Then exp(-inf) = 0, and softmax handles it correctly. Actually, in practice, if I just set masked positions to a large negative number (like `-1e9`), the softmax exp will underflow to 0, giving the same result as `-inf` for post-softmax values. The only potential difference is in the max computation: if all unmasked values are say -100, and masked is -1e9, max is still -100. So no difference. To be completely safe, I can use the exact min float value. For GELU, I need to match HF's NewGELU exactly. OK, I think I have a good plan. Let me now write the full code. Wait, one more thing. In HF GPT-2, the attention mechanism is more complex than what I described. Let me look at the exact GPT2Attention._attn signature. In `transformers/models/gpt2/modeling_gpt2.py`: ```python def _attn(self, query, key, value, attention_mask=None, head_mask=None): attn_weights = torch.matmul(query, key.transpose(-1, -2)) if self.scale_attn_weights: attn_weights = attn_weights / torch.full( [], value.size(-1) ** 0.5, dtype=attn_weights.dtype, device=attn_weights.device ) if not self.is_cross_attention: query_length, key_length = query.size(-2), key.size(-2) causal_mask = self.bias[:, :, key_length - query_length : key_length, :key_length] mask_value = torch.finfo(attn_weights.dtype).min # Need to be a tensor, otherwise we have incorrect attn_weights when padding is max_length mask_value = torch.full([], mask_value, dtype=attn_weights.dtype, device=attn_weights.device) attn_weights = torch.where(causal_mask, attn_weights.to(attn_weights.dtype), mask_value) if attention_mask is not None: # Apply the attention mask attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1) # Downcast (if necessary) back to V's dtype (if in mixed-precision) # Note: _scaled_dot_product_attention already handles this attn_weights = attn_weights.type(value.dtype) attn_weights = self.attn_dropout(attn_weights) # Mask heads if we want to if head_mask is not None: attn_weights = attn_weights * head_mask attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_weights, value) return attn_output, attn_weights ``` During inference without attention_mask and head_mask: 1. Q @ K^T 2. Scale 3. Causal mask 4. Softmax 5. dropout (eval mode: identity) 6. Attn @ V 7. Return So my custom kernel should implement steps 2-6 directly, with steps 1 and 6 being the matmuls. Wait, step 6 is the attn @ V matmul, which I also want to fuse into my custom kernel. So my custom kernel replaces steps 2-6 completely. Actually, in my custom kernel: - Input: QKV [B, S, 3H] - Output: [B, S, H] - Internally: computes attention scores (Q @ K^T), scale, causal mask, softmax, then attention @ V. Yes, this replaces matmul(Q,K), scale, mask, softmax, matmul(attn,V) all with a single kernel that only reads QKV once and writes the final attention output. This is a big optimization! For numerical matching, the only concern is the dot products. Let me use Kahan summation. Also, I need to be careful about the order of operations for scale. HF does: ```python attn_weights = attn_weights / (value.size(-1) ** 0.5) ``` Which is `attn_weights *= (1.0 / sqrt(head_dim))`. In my kernel, I can do `score *= scale` where `scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(head_dim)`. For head_dim=64, `sqrt(64) = 8.0`, so `scale = 0.125`. This is exact in float32. Now, one more detail: the causal mask in HF. `self.bias` is a buffer of shape [1, 1, n_positions, n_positions] where the lower triangle (including diagonal) is True, upper triangle is False. The `torch.where(causal_mask, attn_weights, mask_value)` keeps attn_weights where causal_mask is True, and replaces with mask_value where False. For inference with query_length = key_length = S, `causal_mask = self.bias[:, :, 0:S, 0:S]`. This is the top-left SxS submatrix. It's still the lower triangular mask. In my kernel, I just apply the mask: `if (j > seq_idx) score = NEG_INF`. That's correct. OK, I think I'm ready to write the code. Let me draft it carefully.
assistantI'll analyze the task and write an optimized custom CUDA kernel implementation. The key insight is that at batch_size=1024 and sequence_length=32, we have many relatively small sequences, and the HuggingFace implementation has significant Python/kernel launch overhead. By writing fused custom kernels—especially for attention (which involves ~5 separate kernel launches)—and using direct PyTorch matmuls for the compute-bound GEMMs, we can achieve meaningful speedups. -3.4028234663852886e38f\n\n// Fused embedding: token embedding + position embedding\n__global__ void fused_embedding_kernel(\n const int64_t* input_ids,\n const float* wte,\n const float* wpe,\n float* output,\n int B, int S, int H\n) {\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n int total = B * S * H;\n if (idx >= total) return;\n\n int h = idx % H;\n int s = (idx / H) % S;\n int b = idx / (S * H);\n\n int64_t token_id = input_ids[b * S + s];\n output[idx] = wte[token_id * H + h] + wpe[s * H + h];\n}\n\n// LayerNorm (exact match to PyTorch: unbiased=False, eps=1e-5)\n__global__ void layer_norm_kernel(\n const float* input,\n const float* weight,\n const float* bias,\n float* output,\n int N, int H\n) {\n int n = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n if (n >= N) return;\n\n const float* x = input + n * H;\n float* y = output + n * H;\n\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) {\n sum += x[i];\n }\n float mean = sum / H;\n\n float sq_sum = 0.0f;\n for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) {\n float diff = x[i] - mean;\n sq_sum += diff * diff;\n }\n float var = sq_sum / H;\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f);\n\n for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) {\n y[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i];\n }\n}\n\n// Fused attention: takes [B, S, 3H] from c_attn, outputs [B, S, H]\n// Uses Kahan summation for dot products to improve numerical accuracy\n__global__ void fused_attention_kernel(\n const float* qkv,\n float* out,\n int B, int S, int H, int num_heads\n) {\n int batch_idx = blockIdx.x;\n int head_idx = blockIdx.y;\n int seq_idx = threadIdx.x;\n\n if (seq_idx >= S || batch_idx >= B || head_idx >= num_heads) return;\n\n int head_dim = H / num_heads;\n\n const float* batch_qkv = qkv + batch_idx * S * 3 * H;\n float* batch_out = out + batch_idx * S * H;\n\n // Load Q for this thread's sequence position and head\n float q_val[MAX_HEAD_DIM];\n const float* q_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + head_idx * head_dim;\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n q_val[d] = q_ptr[d];\n }\n\n // Shared memory for K and V of this head for all sequence positions\n extern __shared__ float shared_kv[];\n float* shared_k = shared_kv;\n float* shared_v = shared_kv + S * head_dim;\n\n // Load K and V for this sequence position and head\n const float* k_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + H + head_idx * head_dim;\n const float* v_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + 2 * H + head_idx * head_dim;\n\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n shared_k[seq_idx * head_dim + d] = k_ptr[d];\n shared_v[seq_idx * head_dim + d] = v_ptr[d];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Compute attention scores with Kahan summation\n float scores[MAX_SEQ_LEN];\n float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim);\n\n for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) {\n float dot = 0.0f;\n float c = 0.0f;\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n float y = q_val[d] * shared_k[j * head_dim + d] - c;\n float t = dot + y;\n c = (t - dot) - y;\n dot = t;\n }\n scores[j] = dot * scale;\n }\n for (int j = seq_idx + 1; j < S; j++) {\n scores[j] = NEG_INF;\n }\n\n // Softmax\n float max_score = scores[0];\n for (int j = 1; j < S; j++) {\n if (scores[j] > max_score) max_score = scores[j];\n }\n\n float sum_exp = 0.0f;\n for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) {\n scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score);\n sum_exp += scores[j];\n }\n\n float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp;\n for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) {\n scores[j] *= inv_sum;\n }\n\n // Compute output: weighted sum of V\n float out_val[MAX_HEAD_DIM];\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n out_val[d] = 0.0f;\n }\n\n for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) {\n float w = scores[j];\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n out_val[d] += w * shared_v[j * head_dim + d];\n }\n }\n\n // Write output\n float* out_ptr = batch_out + seq_idx * H + head_idx * head_dim;\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n out_ptr[d] = out_val[d];\n }\n}\n\n// NewGELU activation matching HuggingFace GPT-2\n__global__ void new_gelu_kernel(\n const float* input,\n float* output,\n int N\n) {\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n if (idx >= N) return;\n\n float x = input[idx];\n float c = 0.044715f;\n float sqrt_2_over_pi = 0.7978845608028654f;\n float x3 = x * x * x;\n float t = sqrt_2_over_pi * (x + c * x3);\n output[idx] = 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(t));\n}\n\n// Fused residual add\n__global__ void residual_add_kernel(\n const float* a,\n const float* b,\n float* output,\n int N\n) {\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n if (idx >= N) return;\n output[idx] = a[idx] + b[idx];\n}\n\n// C++ wrappers for Python binding\ntorch::Tensor fused_embedding_cuda(torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor wte, torch::Tensor wpe) {\n int B = input_ids.size(0);\n int S = input_ids.size(1);\n int H = wte.size(1);\n auto output = torch::empty({B, S, H}, wte.options());\n\n int total = B * S * H;\n int threads = 256;\n int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads;\n\n reasoning
The compile failed because the C++ wrapper functions were defined in the .cu file but the pybind wrapper was generated from a .cpp file without proper forward declarations. The issue is that `load_inline` with empty `cpp_sources` doesn't properly expose the CUDA functions. Let me fix by providing a proper `cpp_sources` with forward declarations.

128\n#define MAX_HEAD_DIM 128\n#define NEG_INF -3.4028234663852886e38f\n\n// Fused embedding: token embedding + position embedding\n__global__ void fused_embedding_kernel(\n const int64_t* input_ids,\n const float* wte,\n const float* wpe,\n float* output,\n int B, int S, int H\n) {\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n int total = B * S * H;\n if (idx >= total) return;\n\n int h = idx % H;\n int s = (idx / H) % S;\n int b = idx / (S * H);\n\n int64_t token_id = input_ids[b * S + s];\n output[idx] = wte[token_id * H + h] + wpe[s * H + h];\n}\n\n// LayerNorm (exact match to PyTorch: unbiased=False, eps=1e-5)\n__global__ void layer_norm_kernel(\n const float* input,\n const float* weight,\n const float* bias,\n float* output,\n int N, int H\n) {\n int n = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n if (n >= N) return;\n\n const float* x = input + n * H;\n float* y = output + n * H;\n\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) {\n sum += x[i];\n }\n float mean = sum / H;\n\n float sq_sum = 0.0f;\n for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) {\n float diff = x[i] - mean;\n sq_sum += diff * diff;\n }\n float var = sq_sum / H;\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f);\n\n for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) {\n y[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i];\n }\n}\n\n// Fused attention: takes [B, S, 3H] from c_attn, outputs [B, S, H]\n// Uses Kahan summation for dot products to improve numerical accuracy\n__global__ void fused_attention_kernel(\n const float* qkv,\n float* out,\n int B, int S, int H, int num_heads\n) {\n int batch_idx = blockIdx.x;\n int head_idx = blockIdx.y;\n int seq_idx = threadIdx.x;\n\n if (seq_idx >= S || batch_idx >= B || head_idx >= num_heads) return;\n\n int head_dim = H / num_heads;\n\n const float* batch_qkv = qkv + batch_idx * S * 3 * H;\n float* batch_out = out + batch_idx * S * H;\n\n // Load Q for this thread's sequence position and head\n float q_val[MAX_HEAD_DIM];\n const float* q_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + head_idx * head_dim;\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n q_val[d] = q_ptr[d];\n }\n\n // Shared memory for K and V of this head for all sequence positions\n extern __shared__ float shared_kv[];\n float* shared_k = shared_kv;\n float* shared_v = shared_kv + S * head_dim;\n\n // Load K and V for this sequence position and head\n const float* k_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + H + head_idx * head_dim;\n const float* v_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + 2 * H + head_idx * head_dim;\n\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n shared_k[seq_idx * head_dim + d] = k_ptr[d];\n shared_v[seq_idx * head_dim + d] = v_ptr[d];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Compute attention scores with Kahan summation\n float scores[MAX_SEQ_LEN];\n float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim);\n\n for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) {\n float dot = 0.0f;\n float c = 0.0f;\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n float y = q_val[d] * shared_k[j * head_dim + d] - c;\n float t = dot + y;\n c = (t - dot) - y;\n dot = t;\n }\n scores[j] = dot * scale;\n }\n for (int j = seq_idx + 1; j < S; j++) {\n scores[j] = NEG_INF;\n }\n\n // Softmax\n float max_score = scores[0];\n for (int j = 1; j < S; j++) {\n if (scores[j] > max_score) max_score = scores[j];\n }\n\n float sum_exp = 0.0f;\n for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) {\n scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score);\n sum_exp += scores[j];\n }\n\n float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp;\n for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) {\n scores[j] *= inv_sum;\n }\n\n // Compute output: weighted sum of V\n float out_val[MAX_HEAD_DIM];\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n out_val[d] = 0.0f;\n }\n\n for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) {\n float w = scores[j];\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n out_val[d] += w * shared_v[j * head_dim + d];\n }\n }\n\n // Write output\n float* out_ptr = batch_out + seq_idx * H + head_idx * head_dim;\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n out_ptr[d] = out_val[d];\n }\n}\n\n// NewGELU activation matching HuggingFace GPT-2\n__global__ void new_gelu_kernel(\n const float* input,\n float* output,\n int N\n) {\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n if (idx >= N) return;\n\n float x = input[idx];\n float c = 0.044715f;\n float sqrt_2_over_pi = 0.7978845608028654f;\n float x3 = x * x * x;\n float t = sqrt_2_over_pi * (x + c * x3);\n output[idx] = 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(t));\n}\n\n// Fused residual add\n__global__ void residual_add_kernel(\n const float* a,\n const float* b,\n float* output,\n int N\n) {\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n if (idx >= N) return;\n output[idx] = a[idx] + b[idx];\n}\n\n// C++ wrappers for Python binding\ntorch::Tensor fused_embedding_cuda(torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor wte, torch::Tensor wpe) {\n int B = input_ids.size(0);\n int S = input_ids.size(1);\n int H = wte.size(1);\n auto output = torch::empty({B, S, H}, wte.options());\n\n int total = B * S * H;\n int threads = 256;\n int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads;\n\n fused_embedding_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n input_ids.data_ptr<int64_t>(),\n wte.data_ptr<float>(),\n wpe.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, S, H\n );\n return output;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor layer_norm_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias) {\n int N = input.size(0) * input.size(1);\n int H = input.size(2);\n auto output = torch::empty_like(input);\n\n int threads = 256;\n int blocks = (N + threads - 1) / threads;\n\n layer_norm_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n weight.data_ptr<float>(),\n bias.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, H\n );\n return output;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor fused_attention_cuda(torch::Tensor qkv, int num_heads) {\n int B = qkv.size(0);\n int S = qkv.size(1);\n int H = qkv.size(2) / 3;\n auto output = torch::empty({B, S, H}, qkv.options());\n\n int head_dim = H / num_heads;\n dim3 blocks(B, num_heads);\n int threads = S;\n int shared_mem = 2 * S * head_dim * sizeof(float);\n\n fused_attention_kernel<<<blocks, threads, shared_mem>>>(\n qkv.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, S, H, num_heads\n );\n return output;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor new_gelu_cuda(torch::Tensor input) {\n int N = input.numel();\n auto output = torch::empty_like(input);\n\n int threads = 256;\n int blocks = (N + threads - 1) / threads;\n\n new_gelu_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N\n );\n return output;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor residual_add_cuda(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b) {\n int N = a.numel();\n auto output = torch::empty_like(a);\n\n int threads = 256;\n int blocks = (N + threads - 1) / threads;\n\n residual_add_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n a.data_ptr<float>(),\n b.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N\n );\n return output;\n}\n'''\n\n# Compile inline\ncustom_ops = load_inline(\n name=\"gpt2_custom_ops\",\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=[\n \"fused_embedding_cuda\",\n \"layer_norm_cuda\",\n \"fused_attention_cuda\",\n \"new_gelu_cuda\",\n \"residual_add_cuda\",\n ],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\n\nclass ModelNew(torch.nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, model_name, config):\n super().__init__()\n self.model_name = model_name\n self.config = config\n \n # Load reference model to extract weights\n model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config)\n model.eval()\n \n # Extract GPT-2 weights as buffers\n transformer = model.transformer\n \n self.register_buffer(\"wte\", transformer.wte.weight.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(\"wpe\", transformer.wpe.weight.detach().contiguous())\n \n self.n_layer = config.n_layer\n self.n_head = config.n_head\n self.n_embd = config.n_embd\n self.head_dim = self.n_embd // self.n_head\n \n # Extract per-layer weights\n for i in range(self.n_layer):\n block = transformer.h[i]\n prefix = f\"layer_{i}\"\n \n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_ln1_w\", block.ln_1.weight.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_ln1_b\", block.ln_1.bias.detach().contiguous())\n \n # Conv1D weights: [in_features, out_features]\n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_attn_w\", block.attn.c_attn.weight.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_attn_b\", block.attn.c_attn.bias.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_attn_proj_w\", block.attn.c_proj.weight.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_attn_proj_b\", block.attn.c_proj.bias.detach().contiguous())\n \n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_ln2_w\", block.ln_2.weight.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_ln2_b\", block.ln_2.bias.detach().contiguous())\n \n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_mlp_fc_w\", block.mlp.c_fc.weight.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_mlp_fc_b\", block.mlp.c_fc.bias.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_mlp_proj_w\", block.mlp.c_proj.weight.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(f\"{prefix}_mlp_proj_b\", block.mlp.c_proj.bias.detach().contiguous())\n \n self.register_buffer(\"ln_f_w\", transformer.ln_f.weight.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(\"ln_f_b\", transformer.ln_f.bias.detach().contiguous())\n self.register_buffer(\"lm_head_w\", model.lm_head.weight.detach().contiguous())\n \n del model\n \n def forward(self, x):\n B, S = x.shape\n device = x.device\n \n # Fused embeddings\n hidden_states = custom_ops.fused_embedding_cuda(x, self.wte, self.wpe)\n \n # Process each transformer layer\n for i in range(self.n_layer):\n prefix = f\"layer_{i}\"\n \n # First sublayer: attention\n ln1_w = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_ln1_w\")\n ln1_b = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_ln1_b\")\n attn_w = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_attn_w\")\n attn_b = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_attn_b\")\n attn_proj_w = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_attn_proj_w\")\n attn_proj_b = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_attn_proj_b\")\n \n ln1_out = custom_ops.layer_norm_cuda(hidden_states, ln1_w, ln1_b)\n \n # QKV projection: [B, S, H] @ [H, 3H]\n qkv = torch.matmul(ln1_out, attn_w) + attn_b\n \n # Fused attention: QKV -> attention output [B, S, H]\n attn_out = custom_ops.fused_attention_cuda(qkv, self.n_head)\n \n # Output projection\n attn_out = torch.matmul(attn_out, attn_proj_w) + attn_proj_b\n \n # Residual connection\n hidden_states = custom_ops.residual_add_cuda(hidden_states, attn_out)\n \n # Second sublayer: MLP\n ln2_w = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_ln2_w\")\n ln2_b = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_ln2_b\")\n mlp_fc_w = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_mlp_fc_w\")\n mlp_fc_b = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_mlp_fc_b\")\n mlp_proj_w = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_mlp_proj_w\")\n mlp_proj_b = getattr(self, f\"{prefix}_mlp_proj_b\")\n \n ln2_out = custom_ops.layer_norm_cuda(hidden_states, ln2_w, ln2_b)\n \n # MLP up-projection\n mlp_hidden = torch.matmul(ln2_out, mlp_fc_w) + mlp_fc_b\n \n # GELU activation\n mlp_hidden = custom_ops.new_gelu_cuda(mlp_hidden)\n \n # MLP down-projection\n mlp_out = torch.matmul(mlp_hidden, mlp_proj_w) + mlp_proj_b\n \n # Residual connection\n hidden_states = custom_ops.residual_add_cuda(hidden_states, mlp_out)\n \n # Final layer norm\n hidden_states = custom_ops.layer_norm_cuda(hidden_states, self.ln_f_w, self.ln_f_b)\n \n # LM head: [B, S, H] @ [H, V] This is exactly what `torch.matmul` does with my weight shapes. In my code, `attn_w` has shape `[n_embd, 3*n_embd]`. So `ln1_out @ attn_w` yields `[B, S, 3*n_embd]`. Now, about performance: The attention kernel uses one thread per sequence position per head, with shared memory for K and V. This means for `S=32` and `num_heads=12` (GPT-2 small), we have `B=1024`, `num_heads=12`, `threads=32` per block. So `blocks = (1024, 12) = 12288` blocks. Each block has 32 threads. This gives good occupancy? 12288 blocks total. SMs = ~132 on H100. Each SM can run up to 32 blocks. 12288 blocks / 132 SMs = 93 blocks per SM. That's plenty. But wait, each thread does a lot of computation in a loop over `head_dim=64` (since `n_embd=768`, `num_heads=12`, `head_dim=64`). 32 threads per block means SIMD utilization is fine (warps of 32). Memory access pattern: for K and V loading, each thread loads `head_dim` floats to shared memory. This is coalesced? Yes, because consecutive threads load consecutive elements in K/V? Actually, threads are `seq_idx = threadIdx.x`, and each loads at offset `seq_idx * head_dim + d`. For a fixed `d`, consecutive threads access memory at `seq * head_dim` which are far apart. This is NOT coalesced. For coalesced access, consecutive threads should access consecutive memory locations. Here, thread 0 accesses `k_ptr[0..63]`, thread 1 accesses `k_ptr[768..831]` (if H=768, K starts at H offset). Wait, `k_ptr = batch_qkv + seq_idx * 3 * H + H + head_idx * head_dim`. For consecutive `seq_idx`, the addresses differ by `3*H` floats. So it's strided access. However, in CUDA, a warp of 32 threads issuing 32 loads each of 4 bytes (128 bytes total per access), strided by `3*H*sizeof(float) = 3*768*4 = 9216 bytes`, would be very inefficient. But the global memory loads are not that critical because `S` is only 32, and `head_dim` is 64. The total memory to load per block is `S * head_dim * 2` = `32 * 64 * 2` = 4096 floats = 16KB. That's tiny. The uncoalesced access might not be a huge bottleneck given the small size. But we can improve it. Also, for the Q loading and score computation, each thread reads its own Q values (seq_idx) which are strided similarly. But Q is loaded once per thread. Wait, the main bottleneck in this model is the matmuls, not the attention kernel. Let's estimate: - Embedding: small. - For each of 12 layers: - QKV: [1024, 32, 768] @ [768, 2304]: M=32768, K=768, N=2304. FLOPs = 2 * 32768 * 768 * 2304 = 116 GFLOPs. - Attn Proj: [1024, 32, 768] @ [768, 768]: 2 * 32768 * 768 * 768 = 38.7 GFLOPs. - MLP fc: [1024, 32, 768] @ [768, 3072]: 2 * 32768 * 768 * 3072 = 155 GFLOPs. - MLP proj: [1024, 32, 3072] @ [3072, 768]: 2 * 32768 * 3072 * 768 = 155 GFLOPs. Total per layer: ~465 GFLOPs. Total for 12 layers: ~5.6 TFLOPs. LM head: [1024, 32, 768] @ [768, 50257]: 2 * 32768 * 768 * 50257 = 25.3 TFLOPs. Total: ~31 TFLOPs. On H100, peak FP32 is 67 TFLOPs. But PyTorch matmuls probably use TF32/FP16 Tensor Cores. Wait, default PyTorch autocast might use FP16, but the reference is just `AutoModelForCausalLM` with default settings. The model is loaded in fp32 by default? Actually, `from_pretrained` defaults to `torch.float32`. So it runs in FP32. In FP32, cuBLAS might still use TF32 Tensor Cores on Ampere+ if `torch.backends.cuda.matmul.allow_tf32` is True (which it is by default). So we get ~989 TFLOPS (TF32). Total compute: ~31 TFLOPs at 989 TFLOPS = 31 milliseconds. But 31 TFLOPs in TF32? Wait, the dimensions are not that large. Let's recompute with actual batch: M = batch_size * seq_length = 1024 * 32 = 32768. Gemms are large enough to be compute-bound. But the attention kernel doing manual loops is memory-bound and has overhead. My implementation uses `torch.matmul` for the GEMMs, so those will be as fast as the reference. The main speedup comes from reducing kernel launch overhead and avoiding intermediate allocations. In HuggingFace, each attention involves: 1. layer norm 2. c_attn (matmul) 3. split to q, k, v 4. reshape / transpose for heads 5. matmul Q@K^T 6. apply mask 7. softmax 8. matmul attn_weights @ V 9. reshape / transpose back 10. c_proj (matmul) 11. residual add 12. layer norm 13. mlp fc (matmul) 14. gelu 15. mlp proj (matmul) 16. residual add That's many kernel launches and intermediate tensors. My fused attention kernel does steps 5-9 in a single kernel launch. Similarly, my custom layer norm avoids some dispatch overhead. The residual adds and GELU are single kernels. So even if the CUDA kernels are not perfectly optimized, the fusion alone should provide a speedup by reducing Python overhead and kernel launch overhead. Also note: HuggingFace GPT2Attention has some overhead from `torch.where` and `softmax` on large tensors. My kernel computes softmax per head per sequence position directly in registers/shared memory, which might be faster for small S. One concern: My attention kernel computes softmax in a loop up to `seq_idx` for each thread. For `S=32` and `head_dim=64`, each thread does: - Load Q: 64 reads - Load K and V to shared: 64 reads each - Wait - Compute 32 dot products of 64 elements each: 32*64 = 2048 FMAs - Max over 32: 32 ops - Exp and sum over 32: 64 ops - Weighted sum over V: 32*64 = 2048 FMAs Total per thread: ~4096 FMAs + some memory. A warp of 32 threads: ~131K FMAs. That's decent. But compared to cuBLAS matmuls for [B*num_heads, S, head_dim] @ [B*num_heads, head_dim, S], the cuBLAS approach might be faster for the Q@K^T and attn@V matmuls. However, the small dimensions (S=32, head_dim=64, B*num_heads=12288) might not be perfectly handled by cuBLAS, and the transpose/reshape overheads add up. Let me now write the kernel code with `extra_ldflags` and also try to improve coalescing. Actually, to fix the coalescing issue in the attention kernel: Currently, each thread loads `shared_k[seq_idx * head_dim + d]` for `d=0..head_dim-1`. This means thread 0 accesses `batch_qkv[0*3*H + H + head_idx*head_dim + d]`, thread 1 accesses `batch_qkv[1*3*H + H + head_idx*head_dim + d]`. These are spaced by `3*H` floats. Not coalesced. To make it coalesced, we could have threads cooperate. But with only 32 threads per block and 64 elements, we could have each thread load 2 elements. Or, we can transpose the K and V loading. But this is minor for S=32. Actually, a bigger concern: the `fused_attention_kernel` uses `extern __shared__ float shared_kv[]` with size `2 * S * head_dim`. For GPT-2, S ≤ 1024 (max_position_embedding), but in our case S=32. So shared memory is `2 * 32 * 64 * 4` = 16KB. That's fine. One optimization: instead of using one thread per sequence position, maybe use one warp per sequence position, and parallelize over `head_dim` for dot products. That would make K loading coalesced (since a warp reads consecutive `d` for the same `seq_idx`). But we have `S=32`, so 32 threads per block is already one warp. Using more threads per block (e.g., 256 or 512) and having multiple sequence positions handled by a block would be more complex. Wait, the block size is fixed at `threads = S` because I do `seq_idx = threadIdx.x`. With S=32, that's 32 threads. With `blocks(B, num_heads)`, total threads launched is `B * num_heads * S = 1024 * 12 * 32 = 393,216` threads. That's fine. With `num_heads=12` and `B=1024`, we have `12288` blocks. 32 threads each. Total warps = 12288. On H100 with 132 SMs, that's ~93 warps per SM. Warps per SM limit is 64 for compute capability 9.0? Actually, H100 has a limit of 64 warps per SM? No, it's 2048 threads per SM, which is 64 warps. So 12288 blocks of 32 threads (1 warp each) = 12288 warps total. 12288 warps / 132 SMs = 93 warps per SM. But only 64 warps can be resident per SM concurrently. So some blocks are queued. Still fine. Actually, the occupancy might be limited by registers. For S=32, head_dim=64: `2*32*64 + 32*32 = 4096 + 1024 = 5120` floats = 20KB. This is well under 48KB default shared memory limit. Now, each thread only has scalar variables. I'll write the Python source now. ```python import torch from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # Fix libstdc++ path for import import os if 'LD_LIBRARY_PATH' not in os.environ: os.environ['LD_LIBRARY_PATH'] = '' if '/opt/conda/lib' not in os.environ['LD_LIBRARY_PATH']: os.environ['LD_LIBRARY_PATH'] = '/opt/conda/lib' + os.pathsep + os.environ['LD_LIBRARY_PATH'] cpp_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor fused_embedding_cuda(torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor wte, torch::Tensor wpe); torch::Tensor layer_norm_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias); torch::Tensor fused_attention_cuda(torch::Tensor qkv, int num_heads); torch::Tensor new_gelu_cuda(torch::Tensor input); torch::Tensor residual_add_cuda(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b); ''' cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #define NEG_INF -3.4028234663852886e38f __global__ void fused_embedding_kernel( const int64_t* input_ids, const float* wte, const float* wpe, float* output, int B, int S, int H ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * S * H; if (idx >= total) return; int h = idx % H; int s = (idx / H) % S; int b = idx / (S * H); int64_t token_id = input_ids[b * S + s]; output[idx] = wte[token_id * H + h] + wpe[s * H + h]; } __global__ void layer_norm_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int N, int H ) { int n = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; const float* x = input + n * H; float* y = output + n * H; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) { sum += x[i]; } float mean = sum / H; float sq_sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) { float diff = x[i] - mean; sq_sum += diff * diff; } float var = sq_sum / H; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < H; i++) { y[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i]; } } __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* qkv, float* out, int B, int S, int H, int num_heads ) { int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; int head_idx = blockIdx.y; int seq_idx = threadIdx.x; if (batch_idx >= B || head_idx >= num_heads || seq_idx >= S) return; int head_dim = H / num_heads; const float* batch_qkv = qkv + batch_idx * S * 3 * H; float* batch_out = out + batch_idx * S * H; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* shared_k = smem; float* shared_v = smem + S * head_dim; float* shared_scores = smem + 2 * S * head_dim; for (int d = threadIdx.x; d < head_dim; d += blockDim.x) { shared_k[seq_idx * head_dim + d] = batch_qkv[seq_idx * 3 * H + H + head_idx * head_dim + d]; shared_v[seq_idx * head_dim + d] = batch_qkv[seq_idx * 3 * H + 2 * H + head_idx * head_dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); float* my_scores = shared_scores + seq_idx * S; float max_score = NEG_INF; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { float dot = 0.0f; float c = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float qd = batch_qkv[seq_idx * 3 * H + head_idx * head_dim + d]; float kd = shared_k[j * head_dim + d]; float y = qd * kd - c; float t = dot + y; c = (t - dot) - y; dot = t; } dot *= scale; my_scores[j] = dot; if (dot > max_score) max_score = dot; } for (int j = seq_idx + 1; j < S; j++) { my_scores[j] = NEG_INF; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { sum_exp += expf(my_scores[j] - max_score); } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) { my_scores[j] = expf(my_scores[j] - max_score) * inv_sum; } for (int j = seq_idx + 1; j < S; j++) { my_scores[j] = 0.0f; } for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < S; j++) { acc += my_scores[j] * shared_v[j * head_dim + d]; } batch_out[seq_idx * H + head_idx * head_dim + d] = acc; } } __global__ void new_gelu_kernel( const float* input, float* output, int N ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; float x = input[idx]; float c = 0.044715f; float sqrt_2_over_pi = 0.7978845608028654f; float x3 = x * x * x; float t = sqrt_2_over_pi * (x + c * x3); output[idx] = 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(t)); } __global__ void residual_add_kernel( const float* a, const float* b, float* output, int N ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; output[idx] = a[idx] + b[idx]; } torch::Tensor fused_embedding_cuda(torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor wte, torch::Tensor wpe) { int B = input_ids.size(0); int S = input_ids.size(1); int H = wte.size(1); auto output = torch::empty({B, S, H}, wte.options()); int total = B * S * H; int threads = 256; int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; fused_embedding_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input_ids.data_ptr<int64_t>(), wte.data_ptr<float>(), wpe.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, S, H ); Let me also consider whether `load_inline` might have an issue with `cpp_sources` containing only forward declarations and `cuda_sources` containing the definitions. Since `main.cpp` includes `cpp_sources` (as per the `#include` mechanism in `load_inline`), `main.cpp` will have the declarations. Then `main.cpp` and `cuda.cu` are compiled separately and linked. This is the standard practice and should work. Let me add `#pragma unroll` in the layer norm kernel for potential speedup. Also in the dot product loop. One more thing: `layer_norm_kernel` operates on `float*`. But if input is not contiguous, `.data_ptr()` gives the base pointer but strides would be wrong. I should ensure input is contiguous before passing to the kernel. In my `ModelNew.forward`, I compute `ln1_out = custom_ops.layer_norm_cuda(ln1_out, ...)` where `ln1_out` comes from the previous operation. `torch.matmul` outputs are contiguous. `custom_ops.residual_add_cuda` outputs are contiguous. So tensors passed to layer_norm should be contiguous. But to be safe, I can call `.contiguous()` inside `forward` before layer norm. However, this adds kernel launches. Actually, `torch.matmul` outputs are not guaranteed contiguous in all cases, but for these shapes `[B,S,H] @ [H,N]`, it usually is. To be safe, I can ensure with `.contiguous()` but it might cost time. Let's assume it's contiguous. Wait, `ln1_out = custom_ops.layer_norm_cuda(hidden_states, ln1_w, ln1_b)` receives `hidden_states` from the previous layer. If it's from residual_add, its output is contiguous. The first `hidden_states` comes from `fused_embedding_cuda`, which outputs contiguous. So yes, all are contiguous. Now, one important check: the layer norm kernel uses `int n = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;`. This means each thread handles one sequence position (one row). But in the kernel, I loop over `H` (768 for GPT-2). With `threads=256`, each block handles 256 sequence positions. For `B=1024, S=32`, `N = 32768`. `blocks = ceil(32768 / 256) = 128`. So 128 blocks. Similarly, `fused_embedding_kernel` uses one thread per element. `B*S*H = 1024*32*768 = 25,165,824` elements. `threads=256`, `blocks = ceil(25M / 256) = 98,304`. That's quite a lot! Actually, `25M / 256 = 98,304`. H100 has 132 SMs. Each SM can run up to 2048 threads, so 8 blocks. Total resident blocks = 1056. But we launch 98,304 blocks, which is ~93 waves. That's fine. Wait, but 25M elements means the embedding kernel does 25M loads + 25M adds + 25M stores. That's 300MB of memory traffic. At H100 bandwidth of 3.35 TB/s, that's ~0.09 ms. Very fast. Actually, `fused_embedding` could also be fused with the first layer norm. But it's fine. For `new_gelu_kernel`, `N = 32768 * 3072 = 100,663,296` (for the MLP intermediate). `blocks = 393,216`. Again, many blocks. For `residual_add_kernel`, `N = 32768 * 768 = 25,165,824` each time. `blocks = 98,304`. These elementwise kernels are fine. Now let's think about matmuls. For QKV: `[32768, 768] @ [768, 2304]`. PyTorch `matmul` will dispatch to cuBLAS. This should be very fast. So the overall latency might still be dominated by matmuls. But the fused attention saves several kernel launches and intermediate allocations, and the elementwise fusions save overhead. Let's estimate: - Reference forward pass involves ~100+ kernel launches (embedding, 12 layers * ~8 ops each, layer norm, etc). - Our implementation has: embedding (1), then per layer: layer_norm (1), matmul (1), attention kernel (1), matmul (1), residual_add (1), layer_norm (1), matmul (1), gelu (1), matmul (1), residual_add (1) = 10 kernel launches per layer. Plus final layer_norm (1) and lm_head matmul (1). Total = 1 + 12*10 + 2 = 123 kernel launches. Wait, that's actually not fewer than the reference? Let me count more carefully. Actually, the reference does: For each layer: - ln_1 (1 launch) - c_attn (1 launch for linear = 1 matmul) - split + reshape + transpose (view ops, no kernel launch if contiguous, but sometimes `_conv1d` uses a separate op) - Q@K^T (1 matmul) - mask + softmax (mask is where/add, then softmax = ~2-3 launches) - attn_weights @ V (1 matmul) - reshape + transpose (view) - c_proj (1 matmul) - dropout? (no, eval) - residual add (1 launch) - ln_2 (1 launch) - c_fc (1 matmul) - GELU (1 launch) - c_proj (1 matmul) - dropout? - residual add (1 launch) So that's roughly 12-15 kernel launches per layer. Total 12*12 = 144 + others. Our fused attention replaces: - split/reshape (0) - Q@K^T (0) - mask (0) - softmax (0) - attn_weights @ V (0) - reshape/unreshape (0) with 1 kernel launch. So we save ~5-6 launches per layer. Also, our `residual_add` is one kernel per call. The reference might fuse residual add into the next op or not. It depends. So we save maybe 60-70 kernel launches. At ~5 microseconds per launch, that's 0.3-0.35 ms saved. For a model that takes maybe 5-10 ms total, that's 3-7% speedup. But the attention kernel might also be faster than the separate cuBLAS + softmax for small S. Actually, for `S=32`, the Q@K^T matmul is `[12288, 32] @ [12288, 32].T`? Wait, in the reference, Q and K are reshaped to `[B, num_heads, S, head_dim]`. The matmul is `[B*num_heads, S, head_dim] @ [B*num_heads, head_dim, S]`? Let me check: `torch.matmul(query, key.transpose(-1, -2))` where query is `[B, num_heads, S, head_dim]`. PyTorch handles this as batch matmul. The total batch size is `B*num_heads = 12288`. Matrices are `S x head_dim` and `head_dim x S`, i.e., `32x64` and `64x32`. That's very small matrices. cuBLAS might not be efficient here. So replacing these small matmuls with a fused kernel is likely a big win. Then `attn_weights @ V` is `[12288, 32, 32] @ [12288, 32, 64]`. Also small. So the fused attention kernel could be significantly faster than the reference's cuBLAS approach for these tiny matrices. For MLP, `[32768, 768] @ [768, 3072]` is a single large GEMM (M=32768, N=3072, K=768). This is compute-bound and cuBLAS handles it well. So my implementation should be faster. The compile step itself succeeds (the CUDA code compiles), but the import fails. This is because when Python tries to `import` the generated module, it fails to resolve `CXXABI_1.3.15`. A workaround: maybe I can set `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` via `os.putenv` or use `ctypes.CDLL` to pre-load the correct library. Or, maybe I should compile with `-static-libstdc++` to avoid the shared libstdc++ dependency entirely. Alternatively, since `load_inline` has issues, let me try using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load` to compile the CUDA code as a `.so` with explicit `extra_ldflags` including `-static-libstdc++`. So weight shape is [out_features, in_features]. Wait, actually in the transformers Conv1D implementation: ```python class Conv1D(nn.Module): def __init__(self, nf, nx): super().__init__() self.nf = nf self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(nx, nf)) self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.zeros(nf)) def forward(self, x): size_out = x.size()[:-1] + (self.nf,) x = torch.addmm(self.bias, x.view(-1, x.size(-1)), self.weight) x = x.view(size_out) return x ``` So it's `x @ weight + bias` where weight is [in_features, out_features]. So `matmul(x, weight)` is correct! OK let me try the ctypes preload approach.

sum_exp;\n\n for (int j = 0; j <= seq_idx; j++) {\n my_scores[j] = expf(my_scores[j] - max_score) * inv_sum;\n }\n for (int j = seq_idx + 1; j < S; j++) {\n my_scores[j] = 0.0f;\n }\n\n for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) {\n float acc = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll 4\n for (int j = 0; j < S; j++) {\n acc += my_scores[j] * shared_v[j * head_dim + d];\n }\n batch_out[seq_idx * H + head_idx * head_dim + d] = acc;\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void new_gelu_kernel(\n const float* input,\n float* output,\n int N\n) {\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n if (idx >= N) return;\n\n float x = input[idx];\n float c = 0.044715f;\n float sqrt_2_over_pi = 0.7978845608028654f;\n float x3 = x * x * x;\n float t = sqrt_2_over_pi * (x + c * x3);\n output[idx] = 0.5f * x * (1.0f + bias.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, H\n );\n return output;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor fused_attention_cuda(torch::Tensor qkv, int num_heads) {\n int B = qkv.size(0);\n int S = qkv.size(1);\n int H = qkv.size(2) / 3;\n auto output = torch::empty({B, S, H}, qkv.options());\n int head_dim = H / num_heads;\n dim3 blocks(B, num_heads);\n int threads = S;\n int shared_mem_size = (2 * S * head_dim + S * S) * sizeof(float);\n fused_attention_kernel<<<blocks, threads, shared_mem_size>>>(\n qkv.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, S, H, num_heads\n );\n return output;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor new_gelu_cuda(torch::Tensor input) {\n int N = input.numel();\n auto output = torch::empty_like(input);\n int threads = 256;\n int blocks = (N + threads - 1) / threads;\n new_gelu_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N\n );\n return output;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor residual_add_cuda(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b) {\n int N = a.numel();\n auto output = torch::empty_like(a);\n int threads = 256;\n int blocks = (N + threads - 1) / threads;\n residual_add_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n a.data_ptr<float>(),\n b.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n N\n );\n return output;\n}\n'''\n\n# Compile inline\ncustom_ops = load_inline(\n name=\"gpt2_custom_ops_v5\",\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n Actually, `load_inline` uses `parse_cpp` or similar to extract function signatures from `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`. Looking at PyTorch 2.x source code for `torch/utils/cpp_extension.py`: It builds a `main.cpp` that contains: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> {extra_includes} {extra_cpp_sources} PYBIND11_MODULE({name}, m) {{ {function_bindings} }} ``` Where does it get the function declarations? It usually uses `_import_module_from_library` and the function names are just used in the generated binding code. The generated binding code is: ```cpp m.def("func_name", &func_name, "func_name"); ``` This requires `func_name` to be declared before use. If `cpp_sources` contains the forward declarations, then it works. So I need to put the forward declarations in `cpp_sources`. Let me finalize the code and try with `-static-libstdc++`. Also, since the previous approach had `max_seq_len` as 32 fixed at runtime, but I used `threads = S` which is 32, this is fine for 32. But the shared memory calculation needs to be correct. For `S=32`, `head_dim=64`, `num_heads=12`, shared memory per block = `(2 * 32 * 64 + 32 * 32) * 4 = (4096 + 1024) * 4 = 20480 bytes`. This is trivial. However, I need to make sure `threads = S` is used. For `S=32`, each block has 32 threads, which is one warp. This is not great for occupancy (only 1 warp per SM), but there are `B * num_heads` blocks. For `B=1024`, `num_heads=12`, that's 12288 blocks, which can fill the GPU. But the grid is 2D (`dim3 blocks(B, num_heads)`), which is 1024 x 12. CUDA max grid dimension for x is 2^31-1 and y is 65535, so 12288 blocks in y is too much! `dim3 blocks(B, num_heads)` means `gridDim.x = B = 1024`, `gridDim.y = num_heads = 12`. That's fine, since 12 < 65535. Actually, `gridDim.x = 1024`, `gridDim.y = 12`. Total blocks = 12288. Each block has 32 threads. That's fine. Performance-wise, 32 threads per block is not great for occupancy (max 32 blocks per SM on H100, but each block only has 1 warp, so we get 32 warps per SM, which is actually full occupancy? No, H100 has 128 threads per block max for full occupancy in some contexts, but 32 threads per block means 1 warp, and max 32 blocks per SM -> 32 warps per SM. If each SM supports 64 warps, we only get 50% occupancy. But 12288 blocks / 132 SMs = ~93 blocks per SM, which is enough to hide latency. Still, performance might not be optimal but should still be faster than the reference due to fewer kernel launches and fused attention. Nonetheless, I should consider using larger thread blocks. For attention, I can process multiple sequence positions per block. For example, each block could process a whole head for a batch, with `threads = 128`, where each thread handles `S/threads_seq` positions or each thread handles one element of head_dim. But the current implementation is simpler and should pass correctness. I will optimize if correctness passes but speedup is insufficient. Let me now write the final attempt with `-static-libstdc++`.
